href="/wiki/Yangochiroptera" title="Yangochiroptera">Yangochiroptera</a></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bat_range.png"><img alt="Bat range.png" data-file-height="745" data-file-width="1425" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bat_range.png/220px-Bat_range.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bat_range.png/330px-Bat_range.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bat_range.png/440px-Bat_range.png 2x" width="220"/></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: 88%">Worldwide distribution of bat species\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p><b>Bats</b> are <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <b>Chiroptera</b>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> with their forelimbs adapted as <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">wings</a>, they are the only mammals naturally capable of true and sustained <a href="/wiki/Flight" title="Flight">flight</a>. Bats are more manoeuvrable than <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bat_flight" title="Bat flight">flying</a> with their very long spread-out digits covered with a thin <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">membrane</a> or <a href="/wiki/Patagium" title="Patagium">patagium</a>. The smallest bat, and arguably the smallest extant mammal, is <a href="/wiki/Kitti%27s_hog-nosed_bat" title="Kitti\'s hog-nosed bat">Kitti\'s hog-nosed bat</a>, which is 29&#8211;34&#160;mm (1.14&#8211;1.34&#160;in) in length, 15&#160;cm (5.91&#160;in) across the wings and 2&#8211;2.6&#160;g (0.07&#8211;0.09&#160;oz) in mass. The largest bats are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_fox#Physical_characteristics" title="Flying fox">flying foxes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Giant_golden-crowned_flying_fox" title="Giant golden-crowned flying fox">giant golden-crowned flying fox</a>, <i>Acerodon jubatus</i>, which can weigh 1.6&#160;kg (4&#160;lb) and have a wingspan of 1.7&#160;m (5&#160;ft 7&#160;in).\n</p><p>The second largest order of mammals, bats comprise about 20% of all classified mammal species worldwide, with over 1,200 species. These were traditionally divided into two suborders: the largely fruit-eating <a href="/wiki/Megabat" title="Megabat">megabats</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocating</a> <a href="/wiki/Microbat" title="Microbat">microbats</a>. But more recent evidence has supported dividing the order into <a href="/wiki/Yinpterochiroptera" title="Yinpterochiroptera">Yinpterochiroptera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yangochiroptera" title="Yangochiroptera">Yangochiroptera</a>, with megabats as members of the former along with several species of microbats. Many bats are <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivores</a>, and most of the rest are <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">frugivores</a> (fruit-eaters). A few species feed on animals other than insects; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Vampire_bat" title="Vampire bat">vampire bats</a> <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">feed on blood</a>. Most bats are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnal" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a>, and many roost in caves or other refuges; it is uncertain whether bats have these behaviours to escape <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a>. Bats are present throughout the world, with the exception of extremely cold regions. They are important in their ecosystems for <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinating</a> <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a> and dispersing seeds; many tropical plants depend entirely on bats for these services.\n</p><p>Bats provide humans with some benefits, at the cost of some threats. Bat dung has been mined as <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a> from caves and used as fertiliser. Bats consume insect pests, reducing the need for <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>. They are sometimes numerous enough to serve as tourist attractions, and are used as food across Asia and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Rim" title="Pacific Rim">Pacific Rim</a>. They are <a href="/wiki/Natural_reservoir" title="Natural reservoir">natural reservoirs</a> of many <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a>; and since they are highly mobile, social, and long-lived, they can readily spread disease. In many cultures, bats are popularly associated with darkness, malevolence, witchcraft, <a href="/wiki/Vampire" title="Vampire">vampires</a>, and death.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<p>An older English name for bats is <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/flittermouse" title="wikt:flittermouse">flittermouse</a>, which matches their name in other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> (for example German <i>Fledermaus</i> and Swedish <i>fladdermus</i>), related to the fluttering of wings. <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> had <i>bakke</i>, most likely cognate with Old Swedish <i>natbakka</i> ("night-bat"), which may have undergone a shift from <i>-k-</i> to <i>-t-</i> (to Modern English <i>bat</i>) influenced by Latin <i>blatta</i>, "moth, nocturnal insect". The word "bat" was probably first used in the early 1570s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> The name "Chiroptera" derives from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">&#967;&#949;&#943;&#961;</span> &#8211; <i>cheir</i>, "hand"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> and &#960;&#964;&#949;&#961;&#972;&#957; &#8211; <i>pteron</i>, "wing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny_and_taxonomy">Phylogeny and taxonomy</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Batfossil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="401" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Batfossil.jpg/170px-Batfossil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Batfossil.jpg/255px-Batfossil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Batfossil.jpg/340px-Batfossil.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Batfossil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The early <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> fossil microchiropteran <i><a href="/wiki/Icaronycteris" title="Icaronycteris">Icaronycteris</a></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Green_River_Formation" title="Green River Formation">Green River Formation</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>\n<p>The delicate <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a> of bats do not fossilise well, and it is estimated that only 12% of bat genera that lived have been found in the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> Most of the oldest known bat fossils were already very similar to modern microbats, such as <i>Archaeopteropus</i> (32 million years ago).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons2008_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-simmons2008-8">[7]</a></sup> The extinct bats <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeochiropteryx_tupaiodon" title="Palaeochiropteryx tupaiodon">Palaeochiropteryx tupaiodon</a></i> (48 million years ago) and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hassianycteris_kumari&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hassianycteris kumari (page does not exist)">Hassianycteris kumari</a></i> (55 million years ago) are the first fossil mammals whose colouration has been discovered: both were reddish-brown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bats were formerly grouped in the superorder <a href="/wiki/Archonta" title="Archonta">Archonta</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Treeshrew" title="Treeshrew">treeshrews</a> (Scandentia), <a href="/wiki/Colugo" title="Colugo">colugos</a> (Dermoptera), and <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phylogenetic_relationships_among_recent_chiropteran_families_and_the_importance_of_choosing_appropriate_out-group_taxa_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phylogenetic_relationships_among_recent_chiropteran_families_and_the_importance_of_choosing_appropriate_out-group_taxa-11">[10]</a></sup> Modern genetic evidence now places bats in the superorder <a href="/wiki/Laurasiatheria" title="Laurasiatheria">Laurasiatheria</a>, with its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister_taxon" title="Sister taxon">sister taxon</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fereuungulata" title="Fereuungulata">Fereuungulata</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivorans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pangolin" title="Pangolin">pangolins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odd-toed_ungulate" title="Odd-toed ungulate">odd-toed ungulates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Even-toed_ungulate" title="Even-toed ungulate">even-toed ungulates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetaceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bat_Schematics_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bat_Schematics-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classification_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-classification-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pumo1998_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pumo1998-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhou2011_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou2011-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-16">[15]</a></sup> One study places Chiroptera as a sister taxon to odd-toed ungulates (Perissodactyla).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; background-color: white; vertical-align: middle; width: 248px; margin:0.2em 1em 0.2em 0; float:left; clear:left; class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td border: 0; padding: 0;"><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing Chiroptera within <a href="/wiki/Laurasiatheria" title="Laurasiatheria">Laurasiatheria</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fereuungulata" title="Fereuungulata">Fereuungulata</a> as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister_taxon" title="Sister taxon">sister taxon</a> according to a 2013 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p>The phylogenetic relationships of the different groups of bats have been the subject of much debate. The traditional subdivision into Megachiroptera and Microchiroptera reflected the view that these groups of bats had evolved independently of each other for a long time, from a <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common ancestor</a> already capable of flight. This hypothesis recognised differences between microbats and megabats and acknowledged that flight has only evolved once in mammals. Most molecular biological evidence supports the view that bats form a natural or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons2008_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-simmons2008-8">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; background-color: white; vertical-align: middle; width: 248px; margin:0.2em class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;">Internal relationships of the Chiroptera, divided into the traditional megabat and microbat clades, according to a 2011 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agnarsson_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-agnarsson-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">Genetic</a> evidence indicates that megabats originated during the early <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, and belong within the four major lines of microbats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-16">[15]</a></sup> Two new suborders have been proposed; <a href="/wiki/Yinpterochiroptera" title="Yinpterochiroptera">Yinpterochiroptera</a> includes the <a href="/wiki/Megabat" title="Megabat">Pteropodidae</a>, or megabat family, as well as the families <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_bat" title="Horseshoe bat">Rhinolophidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hipposideridae" title="Hipposideridae">Hipposideridae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craseonycteridae" title="Craseonycteridae">Craseonycteridae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megadermatidae" title="Megadermatidae">Megadermatidae</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhinopomatidae" title="Rhinopomatidae">Rhinopomatidae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teeling-etal-2005_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teeling-etal-2005-19">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yangochiroptera" title="Yangochiroptera">Yangochiroptera</a> includes the other families of bats (all of which use laryngeal echolocation), a conclusion supported by a 2005 DNA study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teeling-etal-2005_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Teeling-etal-2005-19">[18]</a></sup> A 2013 phylogenomic study supported the two new proposed suborders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; background-color: white; vertical-align: middle; width: 248px; margin:0.2em 1em 0.2em 0; float:left; clear:left; width:700px;font-size:85%;line-height:75%">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="border: 1px solid #dedede; padding: 3px">\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r853264556" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg/60px-Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg/80px-Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg 2x" width="40"/></a>\n</p>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">&#8239;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;">Internal relationships of the Chiroptera, with the megabats subsumed within Yinpterochiroptera, according to a 2013 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg/130px-Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg/195px-Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Giant_golden-crowned_flying_fox" title="Giant golden-crowned flying fox">Giant golden-crowned flying fox</a>, <i>Acerodon jubatus</i></div></div></div>\n<p>In the 1980s, a hypothesis based on <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> evidence stated the Megachiroptera evolved flight separately from the Microchiroptera. The <a href="/wiki/Flying_primate_hypothesis" title="Flying primate hypothesis">flying primate hypothesis</a> proposed that, when adaptations to flight are removed, the Megachiroptera are allied to <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> by anatomical features not shared with Microchiroptera. For example, the brains of megabats have advanced characteristics. Although recent genetic studies strongly support the monophyly of bats,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons2008_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-simmons2008-8">[7]</a></sup> debate continues about the meaning of the genetic and morphological evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18367343_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18367343-20">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2003 discovery of an early fossil bat from the 52 million year old <a href="/wiki/Green_River_Formation" title="Green River Formation">Green River Formation</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Onychonycteris" title="Onychonycteris">Onychonycteris finneyi</a></i>, indicates that flight evolved before echolocative abilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> <i>Onychonycteris</i> had claws on all five of its fingers, whereas modern bats have at most two claws on two digits of each hand. It also had longer hind legs and shorter forearms, similar to climbing mammals that hang under branches, such as <a href="/wiki/Sloth" title="Sloth">sloths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">gibbons</a>. This palm-sized bat had short, broad wings, suggesting that it could not fly as fast or as far as later bat species. Instead of flapping its wings continuously while flying, <i>Onychonycteris</i> probably alternated between flaps and glides in the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons2008_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-simmons2008-8">[7]</a></sup> This suggests that this bat did not fly as much as modern bats, but flew from tree to tree and spent most of its time climbing or hanging on branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-norberg_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-norberg-23">[22]</a></sup> The distinctive features of the <i>Onychonycteris</i> fossil also support the hypothesis that mammalian flight most likely evolved in arboreal locomotors, rather than terrestrial runners. This model of flight development, commonly known as the "trees-down" theory, holds that bats first flew by taking advantage of height and gravity to drop down on to prey, rather than running fast enough for a ground-level take off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2011_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2011-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The molecular phylogeny is controversial, as it points to microbats <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">not having a unique common ancestry</a>, which implies that some seemingly unlikely transformations occurred. The first is that laryngeal echolocation evolved twice in bats, once in Yangochiroptera and once in the rhinolophoids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> The second is that laryngeal echolocation had a single origin in Chiroptera, was subsequently lost in the family Pteropodidae (all megabats), and later evolved as a system of tongue-clicking in the genus <i>Rousettus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Analyses of the sequence of the vocalization gene <i>FoxP2</i> were inconclusive on whether laryngeal echolocation was lost in the pteropodids or gained in the echolocating lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> Echolocation probably first derived in bats from communicative calls. The Eocene bats <i><a href="/wiki/Icaronycteris" title="Icaronycteris">Icaronycteris</a></i> (52 million years ago) and <i><a href="/wiki/Palaeochiropteryx" title="Palaeochiropteryx">Palaeochiropteryx</a></i> had cranial adaptations suggesting an ability to detect <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a>. This may have been used at first mainly to forage on the ground for insects and map out their surroundings in their gliding phase, or for communicative purposes. After the adaptation of flight was established, it may have been refined to target flying prey by echolocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-norberg_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-norberg-23">[22]</a></sup> Bats may have evolved echolocation through a shared common ancestor, in which case it was then lost in the Old World megabats, only to be regained in the horseshoe bats; or, echolocation evolved independently in both the Yinpterochiroptera and Yangochiroptera lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Analyses of the hearing gene <i>Prestin</i> seem to favour the idea that echolocation developed independently at least twice, rather than being lost secondarily in the pteropodids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bats" title="List of bats">List of bats</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_fruit_bats" title="List of fruit bats">List of fruit bats</a></div>\n<p>Bats are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placental_mammal" title="Placental mammal">placental mammals</a>. After <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, they are the largest <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a>, making up about 20% of mammal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeiDong2016_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeiDong2016-31">[30]</a></sup> In 1758, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> classified the seven bat species he knew of in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Vespertilio" title="Vespertilio">Vespertilio</a></i> in the order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primates" title="Primates">Primates</a>. Around twenty years later, the German naturalist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Blumenbach" title="Johann Friedrich Blumenbach">Johann Friedrich Blumenbach</a> gave them their own order, Chiroptera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Since then, the number of described species has risen to over 1,200, traditionally classified as two suborders: <a href="/wiki/Megabat" title="Megabat">Megachiroptera</a> (megabats), and <a href="/wiki/Microbat" title="Microbat">Microchiroptera</a> (microbats/echolocating bats).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prothero_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-prothero-33">[32]</a></sup> Not all megabats are larger than microbats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> Several characteristics distinguish the two groups. Microbats use <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a> for navigation and finding prey, but megabats apart from those in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Rousettus" title="Rousettus">Rousettus</a></i> do not, relying instead on their eyesight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_2004_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_2004-35">[34]</a></sup> Accordingly, megabats have a well-developed <a href="/wiki/Visual_cortex" title="Visual cortex">visual cortex</a> and good <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">visual acuity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prothero_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-prothero-33">[32]</a></sup> Megabats have a claw on the second finger of the forelimb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olney_1994_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olney_1994-37">[36]</a></sup> The external ears of microbats do not close to form a ring; the edges are separated from each other at the base of the ear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olney_1994_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olney_1994-37">[36]</a></sup> Megabats <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frugivory" title="Frugivory">eat fruit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nectarivore" title="Nectarivore">nectar</a>, or pollen, while most microbats <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insectivory" title="Insectivory">eat insects</a>; others feed on fruit, nectar, pollen, <a href="/wiki/Piscivore" title="Piscivore">fish</a>, frogs, small mammals, or <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">blood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prothero_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-prothero-33">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3220" data-file-width="2314" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg/170px-Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg/255px-Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg/340px-Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Chiroptera" from <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Kunstformen_der_Natur" title="Kunstformen der Natur">Kunstformen der Natur</a></i>, 1904</div></div></div>\n<p>The following classification from Agnarsson and colleagues in 2011 reflects the traditional division into megabat and microbat suborders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agnarsson_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-agnarsson-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Order Chiroptera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agnarsson_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-agnarsson-18">[17]</a></sup>\n<ul><li>Suborder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megachiroptera" title="Megachiroptera">Megachiroptera</a>\n<ul><li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pteropodidae" title="Pteropodidae">Pteropodidae</a></li></ul></li>\n<li>Suborder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microchiroptera" title="Microchiroptera">Microchiroptera</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Yangochiroptera" title="Yangochiroptera">Yangochiroptera</a> (unranked)\n<ul><li>Family <a href="/wiki/Emballonuridae" title="Emballonuridae">Emballonuridae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Furipteridae" title="Furipteridae">Furipteridae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miniopteridae" title="Miniopteridae">Miniopteridae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molossidae" title="Molossidae">Molossidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Mormoopidae" title="Mormoopidae">Mormoopidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Mystacinidae" title="Mystacinidae">Mystacinidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myzopodidae" title="Myzopodidae">Myzopodidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Natalidae" title="Natalidae">Natalidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noctilionidae" title="Noctilionidae">Noctilionidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllostomidae" title="Phyllostomidae">Phyllostomidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Thyropteridae" title="Thyropteridae">Thyropteridae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Vespertilionidae" title="Vespertilionidae">Vespertilionidae</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rhinolophoidea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rhinolophoidea (page does not exist)">Rhinolophoidea</a> (unranked)\n<ul><li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craseonycteridae" title="Craseonycteridae">Craseonycteridae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Hipposideridae" title="Hipposideridae">Hipposideridae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Megadermatidae" title="Megadermatidae">Megadermatidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhinolophidae" title="Rhinolophidae">Rhinolophidae</a></li>\n<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhinopomatidae" title="Rhinopomatidae">Rhinopomatidae</a></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skull_and_dentition">Skull and dentition</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg/220px-Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg/330px-Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg/440px-Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A preserved megabat showing how the skeleton fits inside its skin</div></div></div>\n<p>The head and teeth shape of bats can vary by species. In general, megabats have longer snouts, larger eye sockets and smaller ears, giving them a more dog-like appearance, which is the source of their nickname of "flying foxes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Among microbats, longer snouts are associated with nectar-feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> while vampire bats have reduced snouts to accommodate large incisors and canines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenhall1983_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhall1983-40">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Small insect-eating bats can have as many as 38 teeth, while vampire bats have only 20. Bats that feed on hard-shelled insects have fewer but larger teeth with longer canines and more robust lower jaws than species that prey on softer bodied insects. In nectar-feeding bats, the canines are long while the cheek-teeth are reduced. In fruit-eating bats, the cusps of the cheek teeth are adapted for crushing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> These feeding behaviors are true for both megabats and <a href="/wiki/Microbat" title="Microbat">microbats</a>. The upper incisors of vampire bats lack <a href="/wiki/Tooth_enamel" title="Tooth enamel">enamel</a>, which keeps them razor-sharp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenhall1983_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhall1983-40">[39]</a></sup> The bite force of small bats is generated through <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_advantage" title="Mechanical advantage">mechanical advantage</a>, allowing them to bite through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sclerotization" title="Sclerotization">hardened</a> armour of insects or the <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">skin</a> of fruit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wings_and_flight">Wings and flight</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bat_flight" title="Bat flight">Bat flight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bat_wing_development" title="Bat wing development">Bat wing development</a></div>\n<p>Bats are the only mammals capable of sustained flight, as opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glide_(flight)" title="Glide (flight)">gliding</a>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Flying_squirrel" title="Flying squirrel">flying squirrel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-molecule_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-molecule-42">[41]</a></sup> The fastest bat, the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_free-tailed_bat" title="Mexican free-tailed bat">Mexican free-tailed bat</a> (<i>Tadarida brasiliensis</i>), can achieve a <a href="/wiki/Ground_speed" title="Ground speed">ground speed</a> of 160 kilometres per hour (99&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner"
