data-framerate="0"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Israeli Bats - 26 September 2015.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Israeli_Bats_-_26_September_2015.webm/220px--Israeli_Bats_-_26_September_2015.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Israeli_Bats_-_26_September_2015.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Israeli_Bats_-_26_September_2015.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Slow-motion and normal speed footage of <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_fruit_bat" title="Egyptian fruit bat">Egyptian fruit bats</a> flying</div></div></div>\n<p>The finger bones of bats are much more flexible than those of other mammals, owing to their flattened cross-section and to low levels of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> near their tips. The elongation of bat digits, a key feature required for wing development, is due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upregulation" title="Upregulation">upregulation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein" title="Bone morphogenetic protein">bone morphogenetic proteins</a> (Bmps). During <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryonic development</a>, the gene controlling Bmp signalling, <i><a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein_2" title="Bone morphogenetic protein 2">Bmp2</a></i>, is subjected to increased expression in bat forelimbs&#8212;resulting in the extension of the manual digits. This crucial genetic alteration helps create the specialised limbs required for powered flight. The relative proportion of extant bat forelimb digits compared with those of Eocene fossil bats have no significant differences, suggesting that bat wing morphology has been conserved for over 50 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sears2006_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sears2006-44">[43]</a></sup> During flight, the bones undergo <a href="/wiki/Bending" title="Bending">bending</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shearing</a> <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stress</a>; the bending stresses felt are smaller than in terrestrial mammals, but the shearing stress is larger. The wing bones of bats have a slightly lower breaking stress point than those of birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As in other mammals, and unlike in birds, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radius_(anatomy)" title="Radius (anatomy)">radius</a> is the main component of the forearm. Bats have five elongated digits, which all radiate around the wrist. The thumb points forward and supports the <a href="/wiki/Leading_edge" title="Leading edge">leading edge</a> of the wing, and the other digits support the tension held in the wing membrane. The second and third digits go along the wing tip, allowing the wing to be pulled forward against aerodynamic <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>, without having to be thick as in <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaur</a> wings. The fourth and fifth digits go from the wrist to the <a href="/wiki/Trailing_edge" title="Trailing edge">trailing edge</a>, and repel the bending force caused by air pushing up against the stiff membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennycuick_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-46">[45]</a></sup> Due to their flexible joints, bats are more manoeuvrable and more dexterous than gliding mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marshall2015_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-marshall2015-47">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="617" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg/220px-Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg/330px-Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg/440px-Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wing membranes (<a href="/wiki/Patagium" title="Patagium">patagia</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Townsend%27s_big-eared_bat" title="Townsend\'s big-eared bat">Townsend\'s big-eared bat</a>, <i>Corynorhinus townsendii</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The wings of bats are much thinner and consist of more bones than the wings of birds, allowing bats to manoeuvre more accurately than the latter, and fly with more lift and less drag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> By folding the wings in toward their bodies on the upstroke, they save 35 percent energy during flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> The membranes are delicate, tearing easily,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ideas_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ideas-50">[49]</a></sup> but can regrow, and small tears heal quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ideas_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-ideas-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> The surface of the wings is equipped with touch-sensitive receptors on small bumps called <a href="/wiki/Merkel_cell" title="Merkel cell">Merkel cells</a>, also found on human fingertips. These sensitive areas are different in bats, as each bump has a tiny hair in the centre, making it even more sensitive and allowing the bat to detect and adapt to changing airflow; the primary use is to judge the most efficient speed to fly at, and possibly also to avoid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stall_(fluid_mechanics)" title="Stall (fluid mechanics)">stalls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sterbing_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-sterbing-52">[51]</a></sup> Insectivorous bats may also use tactile hairs to help perform complex manoeuvres to capture prey in flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marshall2015_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-marshall2015-47">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Patagium" title="Patagium">patagium</a> is the wing membrane; it is stretched between the arm and finger bones, and down the side of the body to the hind limbs and tail. This skin membrane consists of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastic_fibre" title="Elastic fibre">elastic fibres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>. The muscles keep the membrane taut during flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehlhorn2013_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mehlhorn2013-53">[52]</a></sup> The extent to which the tail of a bat is attached to a patagium can vary by species, with some having completely free tails or even no tails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> The skin on the body of the bat, which has one layer of <a href="/wiki/Epidermis" title="Epidermis">epidermis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermis</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Hair_follicle" title="Hair follicle">hair follicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweat_gland" title="Sweat gland">sweat glands</a> and a fatty subcutaneous layer, is very different from the skin of the wing membrane. The patagium is an extremely thin double layer of epidermis; these layers are separated by a connective tissue centre, rich with <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and elastic fibres. The membrane has no hair follicles or sweat glands, except between the fingers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sterbing_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-sterbing-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Makanya2017_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Makanya2017-54">[53]</a></sup> For bat embryos, <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> (cell death) only affects the hindlimbs, while the forelimbs retain webbing between the digits that forms into the wing membranes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015166&#8211;167_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015166&#8211;167-55">[54]</a></sup> Unlike birds, whose stiff wings deliver bending and torsional stress to the shoulders, bats have a flexible wing membrane that can only resist tension. To achieve flight, a bat exerts force inwards at the points where the membrane meets the skeleton, so that an opposing force balances it on the wing edges perpendicular to the wing surface. This adaptation does not permit bats to reduce their wingspans, unlike birds, which can partly fold their wings in flight, radically reducing the wing span and area for the upstroke and for gliding. Hence bats cannot travel over long distances as birds can.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennycuick_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-46">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nectar- and pollen-eating bats can hover, in a similar way to <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbirds</a>. The sharp leading edges of the wings can create <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a>, which provide <a href="/wiki/Vortex_lift" title="Vortex lift">lift</a>. The vortex may be stabilised by the animal changing its wing curvatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roosting_and_gaits">Roosting and gaits</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG/290px-Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG/435px-Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG/580px-Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group of megabats roosting</div></div></div>\n<p>When not flying, bats hang upside down from their feet, a posture known as roosting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenton1984_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenton1984-57">[56]</a></sup> The femurs are attached at the hips in a way that allows them to bend outward and upward in flight. The ankle joint can flex to allow the trailing edge of the wings to bend downwards. This does not permit many movements other than hanging or clambering up trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennycuick_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-46">[45]</a></sup> Most megabats roost with the head tucked towards the belly, whereas most microbats roost with the neck curled towards the back. This difference is reflected in the structure of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cervical_vertebra" title="Cervical vertebra">cervical or neck vertebrae</a> in the two groups, which are clearly distinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenton1984_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-fenton1984-57">[56]</a></sup> Tendons allow bats to lock their feet closed when hanging from a roost. Muscular power is needed to let go, but not to grasp a perch or when holding on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201578_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201578-58">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When on the ground, most bats can only crawl awkwardly. A few species such as the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_lesser_short-tailed_bat" title="New Zealand lesser short-tailed bat">New Zealand lesser short-tailed bat</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Common_vampire_bat" title="Common vampire bat">common vampire bat</a> are agile on the ground. Both species make lateral gaits (the limbs move one after the other) when moving slowly but vampire bats move with a bounding gait (all limbs move in unison) at greater speeds, the folded up wings being used to propel them forward. Vampire bat likely evolved these gaits to follow their hosts while short-tailed bats developed in the absence of terrestrial mammal competitors. Enhanced terrestrial locomotion does not appear to have reduced their ability to fly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_systems">Internal systems</span></h3>\n<p>Bats have an efficient <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>. They seem to make use of particularly strong venomotion, a rhythmic contraction of <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">venous</a> wall muscles. In most mammals, the walls of the veins provide mainly passive resistance, maintaining their shape as deoxygenated blood flows through them, but in bats they appear to actively support blood flow back to the heart with this pumping action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Since their bodies are relatively small and lightweight, bats are not at risk of blood flow rushing to their heads when roosting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bats possess a highly adapted <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a> to cope with the demands of powered flight, an energetically taxing activity that requires a large continuous throughput of oxygen. In bats, the relative alveolar surface area and pulmonary capillary blood volume are larger than in most other small quadrupedal mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Because of the restraints of the mammalian lungs, bats cannot maintain high-altitude flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennycuick_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-46">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2360" data-file-width="1256" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The wings are highly vascularized membranes, the larger blood vessels visible against the light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benhamo_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-benhamo-64">[63]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>It takes a lot of energy and an efficient circulatory system to work the flight muscles of bats. Energy supply to the muscles engaged in flight require about double the amount compared to the muscles that do not use flight as a means of mammalian locomotion. In parallel to energy consumption, blood oxygen levels of flying animals are twice as much as those of their terrestrially locomoting mammals. As the blood supply controls the amount of oxygen supplied throughout the body, the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> must respond accordingly. Therefore, compared to a terrestrial mammal of the same relative size, the bat\'s <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> can be up to three times larger, and pump more blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J&#252;rgens_Bartels_Bartels_1981_pp._243&#8211;260_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-J&#252;rgens_Bartels_Bartels_1981_pp._243&#8211;260-65">[64]</a></sup> Cardiac output is directly derived from heart rate and <a href="/wiki/Stroke_volume" title="Stroke volume">stroke volume</a> of the blood;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martini_2015_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martini_2015-66">[65]</a></sup> an active <a href="/wiki/Microbat" title="Microbat">microbat</a> can reach  a heart rate of 1000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beats_per_minute" title="Beats per minute">beats per minute</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WANG2009_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-WANG2009-67">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With its extremely thin membranous tissue, a bat\'s wing can significantly contribute to the organism\'s total gas exchange efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Makanya2017_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Makanya2017-54">[53]</a></sup> Because of the high energy demand of flight, the bat\'s body meets those demands by exchanging gas through the patagium of the wing. When the bat has its wings spread it allows for an increase in surface area to volume ratio. The surface area of the wings is about 85% of the total body surface area, suggesting the possibility of a useful degree of gas exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Makanya2017_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Makanya2017-54">[53]</a></sup> The subcutaneous vessels in the membrane lie very close to the surface and allow for the diffusion of oxygen and carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a> of bats has varying adaptations depending on the species of bat and its diet. As in other flying animals, food is processed quickly and effectively to keep up with the energy demand. Insectivorous bats may have certain <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">digestive enzymes</a> to better process insects, such as <a href="/wiki/Chitinase" title="Chitinase">chitinase</a> to break down <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, which is a large component of insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> Vampire bats, probably due to their diet of blood, are the only vertebrates that do not have the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Maltase" title="Maltase">maltase</a>, which breaks down <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malt_sugar" title="Malt sugar">malt sugar</a>, in their intestinal tract. Nectivorous and frugivorous bats have more maltase and <a href="/wiki/Sucrase" title="Sucrase">sucrase</a> enzymes than insectivorous, to cope with the higher sugar contents of their diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schondube01_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-schondube01-70">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The adaptations of the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> of bats vary with their diets. Carnivorous and vampire bats consume large amounts of protein and can output concentrated <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>; their kidneys have a thin cortex and long <a href="/wiki/Renal_papilla" title="Renal papilla">renal papillae</a>. Frugivorous bats lack that ability and have kidneys adapted for <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a>-retention due to their low-electrolyte diet; their kidneys accordingly have a thick cortex and very short conical papillae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schondube01_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-schondube01-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patil_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patil-71">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bats have higher metabolic rates associated with flying, which lead to an increased respiratory water loss. Their large wings are composed of the highly vascularized membranes, increasing the surface area, and leading to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cutaneous" title="Cutaneous">cutaneous</a> evaporative water loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benhamo_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-benhamo-64">[63]</a></sup> Water helps maintain their <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte_imbalance" title="Electrolyte imbalance">ionic balance</a> in their blood, <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a> system, and removal of wastes and toxins from the body via urine. They are also susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Blood_urea_nitrogen" title="Blood urea nitrogen">blood urea</a> poisoning if they do not receive enough fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The structure of the uterine system in female bats can vary by species, with some having two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uterine_horn" title="Uterine horn">uterine horns</a> while others have a single mainline chamber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015164_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015164-73">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Echolocation">Echolocation</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation#Bats" title="Animal echolocation">Animal echolocation &#167;&#160;Bats</a></div>\n<table class="mbox-small noprint" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Pipistrellus.ogg" title="File:Pipistrellus.ogg"><i>Pipistrellus</i> pulses</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="5.1911111111111" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Pipistrellus.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_2" preload="none" style="width:220px"><source data-bandwidth="63230" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (63 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Pipistrellus.ogg" type=\'audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"\'/><source data-bandwidth="114672" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/94/Pipistrellus.ogg/Pipistrellus.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Recording of <i>Pipistrellus pipistrellus</i> bat time-expanded echolocation calls and social call.</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr/><span class="selfreference"><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Microbats and a few megabats emit ultrasonic sounds to produce echoes. By comparing the outgoing pulse with the returning echoes, the brain and auditory nervous system can produce detailed images of the bat\'s surroundings. This allows bats to detect, localise, and classify their prey in darkness. Bat calls are some of the loudest airborne animal sounds, and can range in intensity from 60 to 140 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decibels" title="Decibels">decibels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Microbats use their <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a> to create <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a>, and emit it through the mouth and sometimes the nose. The latter is most pronounced in the horseshoe bats (<i>Rhinolophus</i> spp.). Microbat calls range in frequency from 14,000 to well over 100,000&#160;Hz, extending well beyond the range of human hearing (between 20 and 20,000&#160;Hz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muller2004_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-muller2004-76">[75]</a></sup> Various groups of bats have evolved fleshy extensions around and above the nostrils, known as <a href="/wiki/Nose-leaf" title="Nose-leaf">nose-leaves</a>, which play a role in sound transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201531_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201531-77">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animal_echolocation.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="245" data-file-width="318" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/290px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/435px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/580px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animal_echolocation.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Principle of bat echolocation: orange is the call and green is the echo</div></div></div>\n<p>In low-duty cycle echolocation, bats can separate their calls and returning echoes by time. They have to time their short calls to finish before echoes return. Bats contract their middle ear muscles when emitting a call, so they can avoid deafening themselves. The time interval between the call and echo allows them to relax these muscles, so they can hear the returning echo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> The delay of the returning echoes allows the bat to estimate the range to their prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muller2004_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-muller2004-76">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In high-duty cycle echolocation, bats emit a continuous call and separate pulse and echo in frequency. The ears of these bats are sharply tuned to a specific frequency range. They emit calls outside this range to avoid deafening themselves. They then receive echoes back at the finely tuned frequency range by taking advantage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> of their motion in flight. The Doppler shift of the returning echoes yields information relating to the motion and location of the bat\'s prey. These bats must deal with changes in the Doppler shift due to changes in their flight speed. They have adapted to change their pulse emission frequency in relation to their flight speed so echoes still return in the optimal hearing range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to echolocating prey, bat ears are sensitive to the fluttering of <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moth</a> wings, the sounds produced by <a href="/wiki/Tymbal" title="Tymbal">tymbalate</a> insects, and the movement of ground-dwelling prey, such as centipedes and earwigs. The complex geometry of ridges on the inner surface of bat ears helps to sharply focus echolocation signals, and to passively listen for any other sound produced by the prey. These ridges can be regarded as the acoustic equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Fresnel_lens" title="Fresnel lens">Fresnel lens</a>, and exist in a large variety of unrelated animals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Aye-aye" title="Aye-aye">aye-aye</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesser_galago" title="Lesser galago">lesser galago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bat-eared_fox" title="Bat-eared fox">bat-eared fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mouse_lemur" title="Mouse lemur">mouse lemur</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> Bats can estimate the elevation of their target using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_pattern" title="Interference pattern">interference patterns</a> from the echoes reflecting from the <a href="/wiki/Tragus_(ear)" title="Tragus (ear)">tragus</a>, a flap of skin in the external ear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muller2004_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-muller2004-76">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bertholdiatrigona.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2046" data-file-width="2664" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bertholdiatrigona.jpg/220px-Bertholdiatrigona.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bertholdiatrigona.jpg/330px-Bertholdiatrigona.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bertholdiatrigona.jpg/440px-Bertholdiatrigona.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bertholdiatrigona.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bertholdia_trigona" title="Bertholdia trigona">tiger moth</a> (<i>Bertholdia trigona</i>) can <a href="/wiki/Echolocation_jamming" title="Echolocation jamming">jam</a> bat echolocation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corcoran2009_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-corcoran2009-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hristov2005_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-hristov2005-84">[83]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>By repeated scanning, bats can mentally construct an accurate image of the environment in which they are moving and of their prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Some species of moth have exploited this, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctiidae" title="Arctiidae">tiger moths</a>, which produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aposematic" title="Aposematic">aposematic</a> ultrasound signals to warn bats that they are chemically protected and therefore distasteful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corcoran2009_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-corcoran2009-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hristov2005_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-hristov2005-84">[83]</a></sup> Moth species including the tiger moth can produce signals to <a href="/wiki/Echolocation_jamming" title="Echolocation jamming">jam bat echolocation</a>. Many moth species have a hearing organ called a <a href="/wiki/Tympanal_organ" title="Tympanal organ">tympanum</a>, which responds to an incoming bat signal by causing the moth\'s flight muscles to twitch erratically, sending the moth into random evasive manoeuvres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision">Vision</span></h4>\n<p>The eyes of most microbat species are small and poorly developed, leading to poor <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">visual acuity</a>, but no species is blind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Most microbats have <a href="/wiki/Mesopic_vision" title="Mesopic vision">mesopic vision</a>, meaning that they can only detect light in low levels, whereas other mammals have <a href="/wiki/Photopic_vision" title="Photopic vision">photopic vision</a>, which allows colour vision. Microbats may use their vision for orientation and while travelling between their roosting grounds and feeding grounds, as echolocation is only effective over short distances. Some species can detect <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> (UV). As the bodies of some microbats have distinct coloration, they may be able to discriminate colours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-molecule_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-molecule-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang2004_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-wang2004-92">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Megabat species often have eyesight as good as, if not better than, human vision. Their eyesight is adapted to both night and daylight vision, including some colour vision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang2004_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-wang2004-92">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetoreception">Magnetoreception</span></h4>\n<p>Microbats make use of <a href="/wiki/Magnetoreception" title="Magnetoreception">magnetoreception</a>, in that they have a high sensitivity to the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">Earth\'s magnetic field</a>, as birds do. Microbats use a polarity-based compass, meaning that they differentiate north from south, unlike birds, which use the strength of the magnetic field to differentiate <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a>, which may be used in long-distance travel. The mechanism is unknown but may involve <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermoregulation">Thermoregulation</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_bat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="404" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Wiki_bat.jpg/260px-Wiki_bat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Wiki_bat.jpg/390px-Wiki_bat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Wiki_bat.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_bat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thermographic image of a bat using trapped air as insulation</div></div></div>\n<p>Most bats are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeothermic" title="Homeothermic">homeothermic</a> (having a stable body temperature), the exception being the vesper bats (Vespertilionidae), the horseshoe bats (Rhinolophidae), the free-tailed bats (Molossidae), and the bent-winged bats (Miniopteridae), which extensively use <a href="/wiki/Heterothermy" title="Heterothermy">heterothermy</a> (where body temperature can vary).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nowack_2017_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nowack_2017-95">[94]</a></sup> Compared to other mammals, bats have a high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>. The wings are filled with blood vessels, and lose body heat when extended. At rest, they may wrap their wings around themselves to trap a layer of warm air. Smaller bats generally have a higher metabolic rate than larger bats, and so need to consume more food in order to maintain homeothermy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham201199&#8211;100_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham201199&#8211;100-96">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bats may avoid flying during the day to prevent overheating in the sun, since their dark wing-membranes absorb solar radiation. Bats may not be able to dissipate heat if the ambient temperature is too high;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> they use saliva to cool themselves in extreme conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennycuick_46-4"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-46">[45]</a></sup> Among megabats, the flying fox <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pteropus_hypomelanus" title="Pteropus hypomelanus">Pteropus hypomelanus</a></i> uses saliva and wing-fanning to cool itself while roosting during the hottest part of the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ochoa-Acu&#241;aKunz1999_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ochoa-Acu&#241;aKunz1999-98">[97]</a></sup> Among microbats, the <a href="/wiki/Yuma_myotis" title="Yuma myotis">Yuma myotis</a> (<i>Myotis yumanensis</i>), the Mexican free-tailed bat and the <a href="/wiki/Pallid_bat" title="Pallid bat">pallid bat</a> (<i>Antrozous pallidus</i>) cope with temperatures up to 45 Celsius by panting, salivating and licking their fur to promote evaporative cooling; this is sufficient to dissipate twice their metabolic heat production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LichtLeitner1967_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-LichtLeitner1967-99">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bats also possess a system of <a href="/wiki/Sphincter" title="Sphincter">sphincter</a> valves on the arterial side of the vascular network that runs along the edge of their wings. When fully open, these allow oxygenated blood to flow through the <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillary</a> network across the wing membrane; when contracted, they <a href="/wiki/Shunt_(medical)" title="Shunt (medical)">shunt</a> flow directly to the veins, bypassing the wing capillaries. This allows bats to control how much heat is exchanged through the flight membrane, allowing them to release heat during flight. Many other mammals use the capillary network in oversized ears for the same purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Torpor">Torpor</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1980" data-file-width="1408" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG/170px-Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG/255px-Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG/340px-Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tricoloured_bat" title="Tricoloured bat">tricoloured bat</a> (<i>Perimyotis subflavus</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Torpor" title="Torpor">torpor</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Torpor" title="Torpor">Torpor</a>, a state of decreased activity where the body temperature and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> decreases, is especially useful for microbats, as they use a large amount of energy while active, depend upon an unreliable food source, and have a limited ability to store fat. They generally drop their body temperature in this state to 6&#8211;30&#160;&#176;C (43&#8211;86&#160;&#176;F), and may reduce their energy expenditure by 50 to 99%. Around 97% of all microbats use torpor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> Tropical bats may use it to avoid predation, by reducing the amount of time spent on foraging and thus reducing the chance of being caught by a predator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Megabats were generally believed to be homeothermic, but three species of small megabats, with a mass of about 50 grams (1.8&#160;oz), have been known to use torpor: the <a href="/wiki/Common_blossom_bat" title="Common blossom bat">common blossom bat</a> (<i>Syconycteris australis</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Long-tongued_nectar_bat" title="Long-tongued nectar bat">long-tongued nectar bat</a> (<i>Macroglossus minimus</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_tube-nosed_bat" title="Eastern tube-nosed bat">eastern tube-nosed bat</a> (<i>Nyctimene robinsoni</i>). Torpid states last longer in the summer for megabats than in the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">hibernation</a>, bats enter a torpid state and decrease their body temperature for 99.6% of their hibernation period; even during periods of arousal, when they return their body temperature to normal, they sometimes enter a shallow torpid state, known as "heterothermic arousal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> Some bats <a href="/wiki/Aestivation" title="Aestivation">become dormant during higher temperatures</a> to keep cool in the summer months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Heterothermic bats during long migrations may fly at night and go into a torpid state roosting in the daytime. Unlike migratory birds, which fly during the day and feed during the night, nocturnal bats have a conflict between travelling and eating. The energy saved reduces their need to feed, and also decreases the duration of migration, which may prevent them from spending too much time in unfamiliar places, and decrease predation. In some species, pregnant individuals may not use torpor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span></h3>\n<p>The smallest bat is <a href="/wiki/Kitti%27s_hog-nosed_bat" title="Kitti\'s hog-nosed bat">Kitti\'s hog-nosed bat</a> (<i>Craseonycteris thonglongyai</i>), which is 29&#8211;34 millimetres (1.1&#8211;1.3&#160;in) long with a 15 centimetres (5.9&#160;in) wingspan and weighs 2&#8211;2.6 grams (0.071&#8211;0.092&#160;oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edge_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edge-109">[108]</a></sup> It is also arguably the smallest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species of mammal, next to the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_shrew" title="Etruscan shrew">Etruscan shrew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> The largest bats are a few species of <i><a href="/wiki/Pteropus" title="Pteropus">Pteropus</a></i> megabats and the <a href="/wiki/Giant_golden-crowned_flying_fox" title="Giant golden-crowned flying fox">giant golden-crowned flying fox</a>, (<i>Acerodon jubatus</i>), which can weigh 1.6 kilograms (3.5&#160;lb) with a wingspan of 1.7 metres (5.6&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> Larger bats tend to use lower frequencies and smaller bats higher for echolocation; high-frequency echolocation is better at detecting smaller prey. Small prey may be absent in the diets of large bats as they are unable to detect them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gonsalves_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-gonsalves-112">[111]</a></sup> The adaptations of a particular bat species can directly influence what kinds of prey are available to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common_tent-making_bats.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1713" data-file-width="2570" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Common_tent-making_bats.JPG/220px-Common_tent-making_bats.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Common_tent-making_bats.JPG/330px-Common_tent-making_bats.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Common_tent-making_bats.JPG/440px-Common_tent-making_bats.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Common_tent-making_bats.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tent-making_bat" title="Tent-making bat">Tent-making bats</a> (<i>Uroderma bilobatum</i>) in Costa Rica</div></div></div>\n<p>Flight has enabled bats to become one of the most widely distributed groups of mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> Apart from the high Arctic, the Antarctic and a few isolated oceanic islands, bats exist in almost every habitat on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Tropical areas tend to have more species than temperate ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201532_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201532-116">[115]</a></sup> Different species select different habitats during different seasons, ranging from seasides to mountains and deserts, but they require suitable roosts. Bat roosts can be found in hollows, crevices, foliage, and even human-made structures, and include "tents" the bats construct with leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> Megabats generally roost in trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham201121_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham201121-118">[117]</a></sup> Most microbats are <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bats_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-bats-119">[118]</a></sup> and megabats are typically <a href="/wiki/Diurnal_cycle" title="Diurnal cycle">diurnal</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crepuscular" title="Crepuscular">crepuscular</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwab_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwab-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In temperate areas, some microbats <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migrate</a> hundreds of kilometres to winter hibernation dens;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200160&#8211;62_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200160&#8211;62-122">[121]</a></sup> others pass into torpor in cold weather, rousing and feeding when warm weather allows insects to be active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200193&#8211;94_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200193&#8211;94-123">[122]</a></sup> Others retreat to caves for winter and hibernate for as much as six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200193&#8211;94_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200193&#8211;94-123">[122]</a></sup> Microbats rarely fly in rain; it interferes with their echolocation, and they are unable to hunt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_feeding">Food and feeding</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Choeronycteris_mexicana,_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_(7371567444).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1681" data-file-width="2151" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Choeronycteris_mexicana%2C_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_%287371567444%29.jpg/220px-Choeronycteris_mexicana%2C_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_%287371567444%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Choeronycteris_mexicana%2C_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_%287371567444%29.jpg/330px-Choeronycteris_mexicana%2C_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_%287371567444%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Choeronycteris_mexicana%2C_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_%287371567444%29.jpg/440px-Choeronycteris_mexicana%2C_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_%287371567444%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Choeronycteris_mexicana,_Mexican_long-tongued_bat_(7371567444).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mexican_long-tongued_bat" title="Mexican long-tongued bat">Mexican long-tongued bat</a> (<i>Choeronycteris mexicana</i>) drinking from a cactus</div></div></div>\n<p>Different bat species have different diets, including insects, nectar, pollen, fruit and even vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104&#8211;107_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104&#8211;107-125">[124]</a></sup> Megabats are mostly fruit, nectar and pollen eaters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwab_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schwab-120">[119]</a></sup> Due to their small size, high-metabolism and rapid burning of energy through flight, bats must consume large amounts of food for their size. Insectivorous bats may eat over 120 percent of their body weight, while frugivorous bats may eat over twice their weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015116_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015116-126">[125]</a></sup> They can travel significant distances each night, exceptionally as much as 38.5 kilometres (23.9&#160;mi) in the spotted bat (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euderma_maculatum" title="Euderma maculatum">Euderma maculatum</a></i>), in search of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> Bats use a variety of hunting strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gonsalves_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-gonsalves-112">[111]</a></sup> Bats get most of their water from the food they eat; many species also drink from water sources like lakes and streams, flying over the surface and dipping their tongues into the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201576_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201576-128">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Chiroptera as a whole are in the process of losing the ability to synthesise <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22069493_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22069493-129">[128]</a></sup> In a test of 34 bat species from six major families, including major insect- and fruit-eating bat families, all were found to have lost the ability to synthesise it, and this loss may derive from a common bat ancestor, as a single mutation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[b]</a></sup> At least two species of bat, the frugivorous bat (<i>Rousettus leschenaultii</i>) and the insectivorous bat (<i>Hipposideros armiger</i>), have retained their ability to produce vitamin C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21037206_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21037206-131">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span></h4>\n<p>Most microbats, especially in temperate areas, prey on insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104&#8211;107_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104&#8211;107-125">[124]</a></sup> The diet of an insectivorous bat may span many species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015108&#8211;110_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015108&#8211;110-133">[131]</a></sup> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flies" title="Flies">flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, moths, <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">crickets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayflies" title="Mayflies">mayflies</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caddisflies" title="Caddisflies">caddisflies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wray_Jusino_Banik_Palmer_2018_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wray_Jusino_Banik_Palmer_2018-134">[132]</a></sup> Large numbers of Mexican free-tailed bats (<i>Tadarida brasiliensis</i>) fly hundreds of metres above the ground in central Texas to feed on migrating moths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> Species that hunt insects in flight, like the <a href="/wiki/Little_brown_bat" title="Little brown bat">little brown bat</a> (<i>Myotis lucifugus</i>), may catch an insect in mid-air with the mouth, and eat it in the air or use their tail membranes or wings to scoop up the insect and carry it to the mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015120_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015120-137">[135]</a></sup> The bat may also take the insect back to its roost and eat it there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> Slower moving bat species such as the <a href="/wiki/Brown_long-eared_bat" title="Brown long-eared bat">brown long-eared bat</a> (<i>Plecotus auritus</i>) and many horseshoe bat species, may take or <a href="/wiki/Bat_flight#Gleaning" title="Bat flight">glean</a> insects from vegetation or hunt them from perches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> Insectivorous bats living at high latitudes have to consume prey with higher energetic value than tropical bats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fruit_and_nectar">Fruit and nectar</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg/220px-Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg/330px-Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg/440px-Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_fruit_bat" title="Egyptian fruit bat">Egyptian fruit bat</a> (<i>Rousettus aegyptiacus</i>) carrying a <a href="/wiki/Ficus" title="Ficus">fig</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Fruit eating, or frugivory, is found in both major suborders. Bats prefer ripe fruit, pulling it off the trees with their teeth. They fly back to their roosts to eat the fruit, sucking out the juice and spitting the seeds and pulp out onto the ground. This helps disperse the seeds of these fruit trees, which may take root and grow where the bats have left them, and many species of plants depend on bats for <a href="/wiki/Seed_dispersal" title="Seed dispersal">seed dispersal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115-141">[139]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_fruit_bat" title="Jamaican fruit bat">Jamaican fruit bat</a> (<i>Artibeus jamaicensis</i>) has been recorded carrying fruits weighing 3&#8211;14&#160;g (0.11&#8211;0.49&#160;oz) or even as much as 50&#160;g (1.8&#160;oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortega2001_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega2001-142">[140]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nectar-eating bats have acquired specialised adaptations. These bats possess long muzzles and long, extensible <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongues</a> covered in fine bristles that aid them in feeding on particular flowers and plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGtongue_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGtongue-143">[141]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tube-lipped_nectar_bat" title="Tube-lipped nectar bat">tube-lipped nectar bat</a> (<i>Anoura fistulata</i>) has the longest tongue of any mammal relative to its body size. This is beneficial to them in terms of pollination and feeding. Their long, narrow tongues can reach deep into the long cup shape of some flowers. When the tongue retracts, it coils up inside the rib cage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGtongue_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-NGtongue-143">[141]</a></sup> Because of these features, nectar-feeding bats cannot easily turn to other food sources in times of scarcity, making them more prone to extinction than other types of bat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> Nectar feeding also aids a variety of plants, since these bats serve as <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinators</a>, as pollen gets attached to their fur while they are feeding. Around 500 species of flowering plant rely on bat pollination and thus tend to open their flowers at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115-141">[139]</a></sup> Many rainforest plants depend on bat pollination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertebrates">Vertebrates</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GreaterNoctule.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="638" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/GreaterNoctule.JPG/170px-GreaterNoctule.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/GreaterNoctule.JPG/255px-GreaterNoctule.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/GreaterNoctule.JPG/340px-GreaterNoctule.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GreaterNoctule.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Greater_noctule_bat" title="Greater noctule bat">greater noctule bat</a> (<i>Nyctalus lasiopterus</i>) uses its large teeth to catch birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popa_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-popa-147">[145]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Some bats prey on other vertebrates, such as fish, frogs, lizards, birds and mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-5"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015107_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015107-148">[146]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fringe-lipped_bat" title="Fringe-lipped bat">fringe-lipped bat</a> (<i>Trachops cirrhosus,</i>) for example, is skilled at catching frogs. These bats locate large groups of frogs by tracking their mating calls, then plucking them from the surface of the water with their sharp canine teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greater_noctule_bat" title="Greater noctule bat">greater noctule bat</a> can catch birds in flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popa_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-popa-147">[145]</a></sup> Some species, like the greater bulldog bat (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noctilio_leporinus" title="Noctilio leporinus">Noctilio leporinus</a></i>) hunt fish. They use echolocation to detect small ripples on the water\'s surface, swoop down and use specially enlarged claws on their hind feet to grab the fish, then take their prey to a feeding roost and consume it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schnitzler_1994_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schnitzler_1994-150">[148]</a></sup> At least two species of bat are known to feed on other bats: the <a href="/wiki/Spectral_bat" title="Spectral bat">spectral bat</a> (<i>Vampyrum spectrum</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Ghost_bat" title="Ghost bat">ghost bat</a> (<i>Macroderma gigas</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton20014&#8211;5_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton20014&#8211;5-151">[149]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood">Blood</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Desmo-boden_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1403" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Desmo-boden_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Common_vampire_bat" title="Common vampire bat">common vampire bat</a> (<i>Desmodus rotundus</i>) feeds on blood (<a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">hematophagy</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>A few species, specifically the common, <a href="/wiki/White-winged_vampire_bat" title="White-winged vampire bat">white-winged</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hairy-legged_vampire_bat" title="Hairy-legged vampire bat">hairy-legged</a> vampire bats, only feed on animal blood (<a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">hematophagy</a>). The common vampire bat typically feeds on large mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>; the hairy-legged and white-winged vampires feed on birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> Vampire bats target sleeping prey and can detect deep breathing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015119_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015119-153">[151]</a></sup> Heat sensors in the nose help them to detect blood vessels near the surface of the skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_1990_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_1990-154">[152]</a></sup> They pierce the animal\'s skin with their teeth, biting away a small flap,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nowak_1991_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak_1991-155">[153]</a></sup> and lap up the blood with their tongues, which have lateral grooves adapted to this purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup> The blood is kept from clotting by an <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulant" title="Anticoagulant">anticoagulant</a> in the saliva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nowak_1991_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak_1991-155">[153]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Predators.2C_parasites.2C_and_diseases"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators,_parasites,_and_diseases">Predators, parasites, and diseases</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bat-borne_virus" title="Bat-borne virus">Bat-borne virus</a></div>\n<p>Bats are subject to predation from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Owls" title="Owls">owls</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawks" title="Hawks">hawks</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falcons" title="Falcons">falcons</a>, and at roosts from terrestrial predators able to climb, such as cats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RydellSpeakman1995_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-RydellSpeakman1995-157">[155]</a></sup> Twenty species of tropical New World <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> are known to capture bats, often waiting at the entrances of refuges, such as caves, for bats to fly past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esb&#233;rardVrcibradic2007_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esb&#233;rardVrcibradic2007-158">[156]</a></sup> J. Rydell and J. R. Speakman argue that bats evolved nocturnality during the early and middle <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> period to avoid predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RydellSpeakman1995_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-RydellSpeakman1995-157">[155]</a></sup> The evidence is thought by some zoologists to be equivocal so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LimaO\'Keefe2013_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-LimaO\'Keefe2013-159">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_(Greeley_Mine,_cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1432" data-file-width="1216" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg/170px-Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg/255px-Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg/340px-Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_(Greeley_Mine,_cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Little_brown_bat" title="Little brown bat">little brown bat</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_nose_syndrome" title="White nose syndrome">white nose syndrome</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ectoparasite" title="Ectoparasite">ectoparasites</a>, bats carry <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">fleas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, as well as specific parasites such as <a href="/wiki/Bat_bug" title="Bat bug">bat bugs</a> and bat flies (<a href="/wiki/Nycteribiidae" title="Nycteribiidae">Nycteribiidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Streblidae" title="Streblidae">Streblidae</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton2015_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton2015-161">[159]</a></sup> Bats are among the few non-aquatic mammalian orders that do not host <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lice" title="Lice">lice</a>, possibly due to competition from more specialised parasites that occupy the same niche.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton2015_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton2015-161">[159]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_nose_syndrome" title="White nose syndrome">White nose syndrome</a> is a condition associated with the deaths of millions of bats in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">Eastern United States</a> and Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup> The disease is named after a white <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudogymnoascus_destructans" title="Pseudogymnoascus destructans">Pseudogymnoascus destructans</a></i>, found growing on the muzzles, ears, and wings of afflicted bats. The fungus is mostly spread from bat to bat, and causes the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup> The fungus was first discovered in central New York State in 2006 and spread quickly to the entire Eastern US north of Florida; mortality rates of 90&#8211;100% have been observed in most affected caves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_states" title="Mid-Atlantic states">mid-Atlantic states</a> have, since 2006, witnessed entire species completely extirpated and others with numbers that have gone from the hundreds of thousands, even millions, to a few hundred or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> Nova Scotia, Quebec, Ontario, and New Brunswick have witnessed identical die offs, with the Canadian government making preparations to protect all remaining bat populations in its territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup> Scientific evidence suggests that longer winters where the fungus has a longer period to infect bats result in greater mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FrickPollock2010_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FrickPollock2010-169">[167]</a></sup> In 2014, the infection crossed the Mississippi River,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup> and in 2017, it was found on bats in Texas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bats are <a href="/wiki/Natural_reservoir" title="Natural reservoir">natural reservoirs</a> for a large number of <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wong2007_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wong2007-172">[170]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Rabies_in_animals#Bats" title="Rabies in animals">rabies</a>, endemic in many bat populations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McColl2000_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-McColl2000-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calisher2006_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calisher2006-174">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br&#252;ssow2012_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br&#252;ssow2012-175">[173]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Histoplasmosis" title="Histoplasmosis">histoplasmosis</a> both directly and in guano,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2014_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2014-176">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henipavirus" title="Henipavirus">Nipah and Hendra viruses</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EatonBroder2006_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-EatonBroder2006-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halpin2000_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halpin2000-178">[176]</a></sup> and possibly the <a href="/wiki/Ebola_virus" title="Ebola virus">ebola virus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leroy2005_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leroy2005-179">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciAm2006_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm2006-180">[178]</a></sup> Their high mobility, broad distribution, long life spans, substantial <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatry</a> (range overlap) of species, and social behaviour make bats favourable hosts and vectors of disease. Compared to rodents, bats carry more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoonotic" title="Zoonotic">zoonotic</a> viruses per species, and each virus is shared with more species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro2013_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castro2013-181">[179]</a></sup> They seem to be highly resistant to many of the pathogens they carry, suggesting a degree of adaptation to their immune systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro2013_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castro2013-181">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> Their interactions with livestock and pets, including predation by vampire bats, accidental encounters, and the scavenging of bat carcasses, compound the risk of zoonotic transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calisher2006_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calisher2006-174">[172]</a></sup> Bats are implicated in the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Severe_acute_respiratory_syndrome" title="Severe acute respiratory syndrome">severe acute respiratory syndrome</a> (SARS) in China, since they serve as natural hosts for <a href="/wiki/Coronavirus" title="Coronavirus">Coronaviruses</a>, several from a single cave in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>, one of which developed into the SARS virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2014_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2014-176">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2005_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li2005-184">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DrostenHu2017_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-DrostenHu2017-185">[183]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_behaviour">Social behaviour</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_structure">Social structure</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_3" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div
