href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bracken_Bat_Cave" title="Bracken Bat Cave">Bracken Bat Cave</a>, home to 20 million Mexican free-tailed bats</div></div></div>\n<p>Some bats lead solitary lives, while others live in colonies of more than a million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200195&#8211;107_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200195&#8211;107-186">[184]</a></sup> Living in large colonies lessens the risk to an individual of predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-6"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> Temperate bat species may <a href="/wiki/Swarm_behaviour" title="Swarm behaviour">swarm</a> at hibernation sites as autumn approaches. This may serve to introduce young to hibernation sites, signal reproduction in adults and allow adults to breed with those from other groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015188&#8211;189_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015188&#8211;189-187">[185]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several species have a <a href="/wiki/Fission%E2%80%93fusion_society" title="Fission&#8211;fusion society">fission-fusion social structure</a>, where large numbers of bats congregate in one roosting area, along with breaking up and mixing of subgroups. Within these societies, bats are able to maintain long term relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup> Some of these relationships consist of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrilineal" title="Matrilineal">matrilineally</a> related females and their dependent offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup> Food sharing and mutual grooming may occur in certain species, such as the common vampire bat (<i>Desmodus rotundus</i>), and these strengthen social bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter_&amp;_Wilkinson_2013_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carter_&amp;_Wilkinson_2013-190">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_1986_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_1986-191">[189]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1366" data-file-width="1418" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png/220px-Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png/330px-Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png/440px-Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Acoustics of the songs of <a href="/wiki/Mexican_free-tailed_bat" title="Mexican free-tailed bat">Mexican free-tailed bats</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohn_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bohn-192">[190]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Bats are among the most vocal of mammals and produce calls to attract mates, find roost partners and defend resources. These calls are typically low-frequency and can travel long distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-7"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190&#8211;194_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190&#8211;194-193">[191]</a></sup> Mexican free-tailed bats are one of the few species to "sing" like birds. Males sing to attract females. Songs have three phrases: chirps, trills and buzzes, the former having "A" and "B" syllables. Bat songs are highly stereotypical but with variation in syllable number, phrase order, and phrase repetitions between individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohn_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bohn-192">[190]</a></sup> Among <a href="/wiki/Greater_spear-nosed_bat" title="Greater spear-nosed bat">greater spear-nosed bats</a> (<i>Phyllostomus hastatus</i>), females produce loud, broadband calls among their roost mates to form group cohesion. Calls differ between roosting groups and may arise from vocal learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a study on captive Egyptian fruit bats, 70% of the directed calls could be identified by the researchers as to which individual bat made it, and 60% could be categorised into four contexts: squabbling over food, jostling over position in their sleeping cluster, protesting over mating attempts and arguing when perched in close proximity to each other. The animals made slightly different sounds when communicating with different individual bats, especially those of the opposite sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> In the highly <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexually dimorphic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hammer-headed_bat" title="Hammer-headed bat">hammer-headed bat</a> (<i>Hypsignathus monstrosus</i>), males produce deep, resonating, monotonous calls to attract females. Bats in flight make vocal signals for traffic control. Greater bulldog bats honk when on a collision course with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190&#8211;194_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190&#8211;194-193">[191]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bats also communicate by other means. Male <a href="/wiki/Little_yellow-shouldered_bat" title="Little yellow-shouldered bat">little yellow-shouldered bats</a> (<i>Sturnira lilium</i>) have shoulder glands that produce a spicy odour during the breeding season. Like many other species, they have hair specialised for retaining and dispersing secretions. Such hair forms a conspicuous collar around the necks of the some Old World megabat males. Male <a href="/wiki/Greater_sac-winged_bat" title="Greater sac-winged bat">greater sac-winged bats</a> (<i>Saccopteryx bilineata</i>) have sacs in their wings in which they mix body secretions like saliva and urine to create a perfume that they sprinkle on roost sites, a behaviour known as "salting". Salting may be accompanied by singing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190&#8211;194_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190&#8211;194-193">[191]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_life_history">Reproduction and life history</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1424" data-file-width="1904" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group of polygynous vampire bats</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategies">Strategies</span></h3>\n<p>Most bat species are <a href="/wiki/Polygyny_in_animals" title="Polygyny in animals">polygynous</a>, where males mate with multiple females. Male pipistrelle, noctule and vampire bats may claim and defend resources that attract females, such as roost sites, and mate with those females. Males unable to claim a site are forced to live on the periphery where they have less reproductive success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mating_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-mating-196">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-8"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Promiscuity" title="Promiscuity">Promiscuity</a>, where both sexes mate with multiple partners, exists in species like the Mexican free-tailed bat and the little brown bat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> There appears to be bias towards certain males among females in these bats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-9"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> In a few species, such as the yellow-winged bat and spectral bat, adult males and females form <a href="/wiki/Monogamy_in_animals" title="Monogamy in animals">monogamous</a> pairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-10"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015197_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015197-199">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lek_mating" title="Lek mating">Lek mating</a>, where males aggregate and compete for female choice through display, is rare in bats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup> but occurs in the hammerheaded bat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mating">Mating</span></h3>\n<p>For temperate living bats, mating takes place in late summer and early autumn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011105_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011105-202">[200]</a></sup> Tropical bats may mate during the dry season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup> After copulation, the male may leave behind a <a href="/wiki/Mating_plug" title="Mating plug">mating plug</a> to block the sperm of other males and thus ensure his paternity. In hibernating species, males are known to mate with females in torpor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-11"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> Female bats use a variety of strategies to control the timing of pregnancy and the birth of young, to make delivery coincide with maximum food ability and other ecological factors. Females of some species have delayed fertilisation, in which sperm is stored in the reproductive tract for several months after mating. Mating occurs in the autumn but fertilisation does not occur until the following spring. Other species exhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delayed_implantation" title="Delayed implantation">delayed implantation</a>, in which the egg is fertilised after mating, but remains free in the reproductive tract until external conditions become favourable for giving birth and caring for the offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011114&#8211;119_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011114&#8211;119-204">[202]</a></sup> In another strategy, fertilisation and implantation both occur, but development of the foetus is delayed until good conditions prevail. During the delayed development the mother keeps the fertilised egg alive with nutrients. This process can go on for a long period, because of the advanced gas exchange system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg/220px-Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg/330px-Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg/440px-Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newborn common pipistrelle, <i>Pipistrellus pipistrellus</i></div></div></div>\n<p>For temperate living bats, births typically take place in May or June in the northern hemisphere; births in the southern hemisphere occur in November and December. Tropical species give birth at the beginning of the rainy season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> In most bat species, females carry and give birth to a single pup per litter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup> At birth, a bat pup can be up to 40 percent of the mother\'s weight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-12"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> and the pelvic girdle of the female can expand during birth as the two halves are connected by a flexible ligament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166-208">[206]</a></sup> Females typically give birth in a head-up or horizontal position, using gravity to make birthing easier. The young emerges rear-first, possibly to prevent the wings from getting tangled, and the female cradles it in her wing and tail membranes. In many species, females give birth and raise their young in <a href="/wiki/Maternity_colony_(bats)" title="Maternity colony (bats)">maternity colonies</a> and may assist each other in birthing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011119_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011119-210">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166-208">[206]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most of the care for a young bat comes from the mother. In monogamous species, the father plays a role. Allo-suckling, where a female suckles another mother\'s young, occurs in several species. This may serve to increase colony size in species where females return to their natal colony to breed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_39-13"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-39">[38]</a></sup> A young bat\'s ability to fly coincides with the development of an adult body and forelimb length. For the little brown bat, this occurs about eighteen days after birth. Weaning of young for most species takes place in under eighty days. The common vampire bat nurses its offspring beyond that and young vampire bats achieve independence later in life than other species. This is probably due to the species\' blood-based diet, which is difficult to obtain on a nightly basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015171_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015171-211">[209]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_expectancy">Life expectancy</span></h3>\n<p>The maximum lifespan of bats is three-and-a-half times longer than other mammals of similar size. Six species have been recorded to live over 30 years in the wild: the brown long-eared bat (<i>Plecotus auritus</i>), the little brown bat <i>(Myotis lucifugus)</i>, <a href="/wiki/Brandt%27s_bat" title="Brandt\'s bat">Brandt\'s bat</a> (<i>Myotis brandti</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_mouse-eared_bat" title="Lesser mouse-eared bat">lesser mouse-eared bat</a> (<i>Myotis blythii</i>) the <a href="/wiki/Greater_horseshoe_bat" title="Greater horseshoe bat">greater horseshoe bat</a> (<i>Rhinolophus ferrumequinum</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_flying_fox" title="Indian flying fox">Indian flying fox</a> (<i>Pteropus giganteus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> One hypothesis consistent with the <a href="/wiki/Rate-of-living_theory" title="Rate-of-living theory">rate-of-living theory</a> links this to the fact that they slow down their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolic_rate" title="Metabolic rate">metabolic rate</a> while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hibernating" title="Hibernating">hibernating</a>; bats that hibernate, on average, have a longer lifespan than bats that do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkinson2002_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson2002-214">[212]</a></sup> Another hypothesis is that flying has reduced their mortality rate, which would also be true for birds and gliding mammals. Bat species that give birth to multiple pups generally have a shorter lifespan than species that give birth to only a single pup. Cave-roosting species may have a longer lifespan than non-roosting species because of the decreased predation in caves. A male Brandt\'s bat was recaptured in the wild after 41 years, making it the oldest known bat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkinson2002_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson2002-214">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions_with_humans">Interactions with humans</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bats_by_population" title="List of bats by population">List of bats by population</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1265" data-file-width="964" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg/170px-Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg/255px-Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg/340px-Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bat_Roost_San_Antonio_Texas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bat roost in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Antonio,_Texas" title="San Antonio, Texas">San Antonio, Texas</a>, 1915</div></div></div>\n<p>Groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Bat_Conservation_International" title="Bat Conservation International">Bat Conservation International</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup> aim to increase awareness of bats\' ecological roles and the environmental threats they face. In the United Kingdom, all bats are protected under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wildlife_and_Countryside_Act" title="Wildlife and Countryside Act">Wildlife and Countryside Acts</a>, and disturbing a bat or its roost can be punished with a heavy fine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup>\nIn <a href="/wiki/Sarawak" title="Sarawak">Sarawak</a>, Malaysia, "all bats"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPO1998_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPO1998-218">[216]</a></sup> are protected under the <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_Wildlife_Law" title="Malaysian Wildlife Law">Wildlife Protection Ordinance 1998</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPO1998_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPO1998-218">[216]</a></sup> but species such as the <a href="/wiki/Hairless_bat" title="Hairless bat">hairless bat</a> (<i>Cheiromeles torquatus)</i> are still eaten by the local communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup> Humans have caused the extinction of several species of bat in modern history, the most recent being the <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Island_pipistrelle" title="Christmas Island pipistrelle">Christmas Island pipistrelle</a> (<i>Pipistrellus murrayi</i>), which was declared extinct in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many people put up bat houses to attract bats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup> The 1991 <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> bat house is the largest occupied artificial roost in the world, with around 400,000 residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup> In Britain, thickwalled and partly underground <a href="/wiki/British_hardened_field_defences_of_World_War_II" title="British hardened field defences of World War II">World War II pillboxes</a> have been converted to make roosts for bats,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup> and purpose-built bat houses are occasionally built to mitigate damage to habitat from road or other developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cave_gate" title="Cave gate">Cave gates</a> are sometimes installed to limit human entry into caves with sensitive or endangered bat species. The gates are designed not to limit the airflow, and thus to maintain the cave\'s micro-ecosystem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGENCY_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGENCY-227">[225]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bat_as_food" title="Bat as food">Bats are eaten</a> in countries across Asia and the Pacific Rim. In some cases, such as in Guam, flying foxes have become endangered through being hunted for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup> There is evidence that <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> create sufficient <a href="/wiki/Barotrauma" title="Barotrauma">barotrauma</a> (pressure damage) to kill bats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaerwaldD\'Amours2008_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaerwaldD\'Amours2008-229">[227]</a></sup> Bats have typical <a href="/wiki/Lung#Mammalian_lungs" title="Lung">mammalian lungs</a>, which are thought to be more sensitive to sudden air pressure changes than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avian_lungs" title="Avian lungs">the lungs of birds</a>, making them more liable to fatal rupture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nww_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-nww-230">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[232]</a></sup> Bats may be attracted to turbines, perhaps seeking roosts, increasing the death rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nww_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-nww-230">[228]</a></sup> Acoustic deterrents may help to reduce bat mortality at wind farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_(No._43),_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="2645" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_(No._43),_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Francisco_Goya" title="Francisco Goya">Francisco Goya</a>, <i>The Sleep of Reason Produces Monsters</i>, 1797</div></div></div>\n<p>Since bats are mammals, yet can fly, they are considered to be <a href="/wiki/Liminal_being" title="Liminal being">liminal beings</a> in various traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup> In many cultures, including in Europe, bats are associated with darkness, death, witchcraft, and malevolence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbols_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-symbols-237">[235]</a></sup> Among <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creek_(people)" title="Creek (people)">Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apache_Tribe" title="Apache Tribe">Apache</a>, the bat is a <a href="/wiki/Trickster" title="Trickster">trickster</a> spirit. In Tanzania, a winged batlike creature known as <a href="/wiki/Popobawa" title="Popobawa">Popobawa</a> is believed to be a shapeshifting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evil_spirit" title="Evil spirit">evil spirit</a> that assaults and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodomises" title="Sodomises">sodomises</a> its victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>, bats symbolised the land of the dead, destruction, and decay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReadGonzalez_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReadGonzalez-240">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup> An East Nigerian tale tells that the bat developed its nocturnal habits after causing the death of his partner, the bush-rat, and now hides by day to avoid arrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnott1962_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnott1962-242">[240]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More positive depictions of bats exist in some cultures. In China, bats have been associated with happiness, joy and good fortune. Five bats are used to symbolise the "Five Blessings": longevity, wealth, health, love of virtue and peaceful death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[241]</a></sup> The bat is sacred in <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a> and is often considered the physical manifestation of a separable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilisation" title="Zapotec civilisation">Zapotec civilisation</a> of Mesoamerica, the bat god presided over corn and fertility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bat_god,_Zapotec,_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4320" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG/170px-Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG/255px-Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG/340px-Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bat_god,_Zapotec,_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilization" title="Zapotec civilization">Zapotec</a> bat god, <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a>, 350&#8211;500 AD</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weird_Sisters" title="Weird Sisters">Weird Sisters</a> in Shakespeare\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i> used the fur of a bat in their brew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deVries76_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-246">[244]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, the bat is often a symbol of the night and its foreboding nature. The bat is a primary animal associated with fictional characters of the night, both <a href="/wiki/Villain" title="Villain">villainous</a> <a href="/wiki/Vampire" title="Vampire">vampires</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Count_Dracula" title="Count Dracula">Count Dracula</a> and before him <i><a href="/wiki/Varney_the_Vampire" title="Varney the Vampire">Varney the Vampire</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hero" title="Hero">heroes</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleisher_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fleisher-248">[246]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Oppel" title="Kenneth Oppel">Kenneth Oppel</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Silverwing_(series)" title="Silverwing (series)">Silverwing novels</a> narrate the adventures of a young bat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[247]</a></sup> based on the <a href="/wiki/Silver-haired_bat" title="Silver-haired bat">silver-haired bat</a> of North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The bat is sometimes used as a <a href="/wiki/Bat_(heraldry)" title="Bat (heraldry)">heraldic symbol</a> in Spain and France, appearing in the coats of arms of the towns of <a href="/wiki/Valencia" title="Valencia">Valencia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palma_de_Mallorca" title="Palma de Mallorca">Palma de Mallorca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraga" title="Fraga">Fraga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albacete" title="Albacete">Albacete</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Montchauvet,_Yvelines" title="Montchauvet, Yvelines">Montchauvet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cervantesvirtual.com_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-cervantesvirtual.com-251">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifc.dpz.es_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-ifc.dpz.es-253">[251]</a></sup> Three US states have an official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_bats" title="List of U.S. state bats">state bat</a>. Texas and Oklahoma are represented by the Mexican free-tailed bat, while Virginia is represented by the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_big-eared_bat" title="Virginia big-eared bat">Virginia big-eared bat</a> (<i>Corynorhinus townsendii virginianus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>\n<p>Insectivorous bats in particular are especially helpful to farmers, as they control populations of agricultural pests and reduce the need to use <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>. It has been estimated that bats save the agricultural industry of the United States anywhere from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">$</a>3.7 billion to $53 billion per year in pesticides and damage to crops. This also prevents the overuse of pesticides, which can pollute the surrounding environment, and may lead to resistance in future generations of insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bat dung, a type of <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a>, is rich in nitrates and is mined from caves for use as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertiliser" title="Fertiliser">fertiliser</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Civil_War" title="US Civil War">US Civil War</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltpetre" title="Saltpetre">saltpetre</a> was collected from caves to make <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>; it used to be thought that this was bat guano, but most of the nitrate comes from <a href="/wiki/Nitrifying_bacteria" title="Nitrifying bacteria">nitrifying bacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ann_W._Richards_Congress_Avenue_Bridge" title="Ann W. Richards Congress Avenue Bridge">Congress Avenue Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>, is the summer home to North America\'s largest urban bat colony, an estimated 1,500,000 Mexican free-tailed bats. About 100,000 tourists a year visit the bridge at twilight to watch the bats leave the roost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portal-puzzle.svg"><img alt="Portal-puzzle.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/16px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/24px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/32px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Bats" title="Portal:Bats">Bats portal</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bat_detector" title="Bat detector">Bat detector</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/a&#618;/: \'i\' in \'tide\'">a&#618;</span><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'r\' in \'rye\'">r</span><span title="/&#594;/: \'o\' in \'body\'">&#594;</span><span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">p</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span><span title="/&#601;r/: \'er\' in \'letter\'">&#601;r</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span></span>/</a></span></span>; from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">&#967;&#949;&#943;&#961;</span> &#8211; <i>cheir</i>, "hand" and &#960;&#964;&#949;&#961;&#972;&#957; &#8211; <i>pteron</i>, "wing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-1">[1]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Earlier reports that only fruit bats were deficient were based on smaller samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21037206_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21037206-131">[130]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline"
